The exiled Jews fled to Ctesiphon, Nehardea, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nisibis" title="Nisibis">Nisibis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg/220px-Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg/330px-Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg/440px-Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Denarius_19_BC_2230399.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> struck in 19&#160;BC during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, with the goddess <a href="/wiki/Feronia_(mythology)" title="Feronia (mythology)">Feronia</a> depicted on the obverse, and on the reverse a Parthian man kneeling in submission while offering the Roman <a href="/wiki/Aquila_(Roman)" title="Aquila (Roman)">military standards</a> taken at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Although at peace with Parthia, Rome still interfered in its affairs. The Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> (r.&#160;14&#8211;37&#160;AD) became involved in a plot by <a href="/wiki/Pharasmanes_I_of_Iberia" title="Pharasmanes I of Iberia">Pharasmanes I of Iberia</a> to place his brother <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_of_Armenia" title="Mithridates of Armenia">Mithridates</a> on the throne of Armenia by assassinating the Parthian ally King Arsaces of Armenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Artabanus III tried and failed to restore Parthian control of Armenia, prompting an aristocratic revolt that forced him to flee to <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>. The Romans released a hostage prince, <a href="/wiki/Tiridates_III_of_Parthia" title="Tiridates III of Parthia">Tiridates III of Parthia</a>, to rule the region as an ally of Rome. Shortly before his death, Artabanus managed to force Tiridates from the throne using troops from Hyrcania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> After Artabanus\' death in 38&#160;AD, a long civil war ensued between the rightful successor <a href="/wiki/Vardanes_I" title="Vardanes I">Vardanes I</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Gotarzes_II_of_Parthia" title="Gotarzes II of Parthia">Gotarzes II of Parthia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> After Vardanes was assassinated during a hunting expedition, the Parthian nobility appealed to Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> (<i>r</i>. 41&#8211;54&#160;AD) in 49&#160;AD to release the hostage prince Meherdates to challenge Gotarzes. This backfired when Meherdates was betrayed by the governor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edessa,_Mesopotamia" title="Edessa, Mesopotamia">Edessa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Izates_bar_Monobaz" title="Izates bar Monobaz">Izates bar Monobaz</a> of <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>; he was captured and sent to Gotarzes, where he was allowed to live after having his ears mutilated, an act that disqualified him from inheriting the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 97&#160;AD, the Chinese general <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">Protector-General of the Western Regions</a>, sent his emissary <a href="/wiki/Gan_Ying" title="Gan Ying">Gan Ying</a> on a diplomatic mission to reach the Roman Empire. Gan visited the court of <a href="/wiki/Pacorus_II" title="Pacorus II">Pacorus II</a> at Hecatompylos before departing towards Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watson_1983_543-544_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-watson_1983_543-544-121">[121]</a></sup> He traveled as far west as the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, where Parthian authorities convinced him that an arduous sea voyage around the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> was the only means to reach Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Discouraged by this, Gan Ying returned to the Han court and provided <a href="/wiki/Emperor_He_of_Han" title="Emperor He of Han">Emperor He of Han</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;88&#8211;105&#160;AD) with a detailed report on the Roman Empire based on oral accounts of his Parthian hosts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> William Watson speculates that the Parthians would have been relieved at the failed efforts by the Han Empire to open diplomatic relations with Rome, especially after Ban Chao\'s <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han&#8211;Xiongnu War">military victories</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">eastern Central Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watson_1983_543-544_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-watson_1983_543-544-121">[121]</a></sup> However, Chinese records maintain that a <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Roman embassy</a>, perhaps only a group of <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Roman merchants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">arrived at the Han capital</a> <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> by way of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> (northern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>) in 166&#160;AD, during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> (<i>r</i>. 161&#8211;180&#160;AD) and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huan_of_Han" title="Emperor Huan of Han">Emperor Huan of Han</a> (<i>r</i>. 146&#8211;168&#160;AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Although it could be coincidental, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonine" title="Antonine">Antonine</a> Roman golden <a href="/wiki/Medal" title="Medal">medallions</a> dated to the reigns of Marcus Aurelius and his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> have been discovered at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oc_Eo" title="Oc Eo">Oc Eo</a>, Vietnam (among other Roman artefacts in the <a href="/wiki/Mekong_Delta" title="Mekong Delta">Mekong Delta</a>), a site that is one of the suggested locations for the port city of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattigara" title="Cattigara">Cattigara</a>" along the <i><a href="/wiki/Magnus_Sinus" title="Magnus Sinus">Magnus Sinus</a></i> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand">Gulf of Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuation_of_Roman_hostilities_and_Parthian_decline">Continuation of Roman hostilities and Parthian decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Continuation of Roman hostilities and Parthian decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_58%E2%80%9363" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Parthian_campaign" title="Trajan\'s Parthian campaign">Trajan\'s Parthian campaign</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_161%E2%80%93166" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 161&#8211;166">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 161&#8211;166</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parthian_war_of_Caracalla" title="Parthian war of Caracalla">Parthian war of Caracalla</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Armenia" title="Roman Armenia">Roman Armenia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2025" data-file-width="3350" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg/300px-Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg/450px-Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg/600px-Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman-Parthian_War_58-60.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the troop movements during the first two years of the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_58%E2%80%9363" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63</a> AD over the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a>, detailing the Roman offensive into <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and capture of the country by <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Domitius_Corbulo" title="Gnaeus Domitius Corbulo">Gnaeus Domitius Corbulo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasian_Iberia" title="Caucasian Iberia">Iberian king</a> Pharasmanes I had his son <a href="/wiki/Rhadamistus" title="Rhadamistus">Rhadamistus</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;51&#8211;55&#160;AD) invade Armenia to depose the Roman client king Mithridates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vologeses_I_of_Parthia" title="Vologeses I of Parthia">Vologeses I of Parthia</a> (<i>r</i>. c.&#160;51&#8211;77&#160;AD) planned to invade and place his brother, the later <a href="/wiki/Tiridates_I_of_Armenia" title="Tiridates I of Armenia">Tiridates I of Armenia</a>, on the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Rhadamistus was eventually driven from power, and, beginning with the reign of Tiridates, Parthia would retain firm control over Armenia&#8212;with brief interruptions&#8212;through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arsacid_Dynasty_of_Armenia" title="Arsacid Dynasty of Armenia">Arsacid Dynasty of Armenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Even after the fall of the Parthian Empire, the Arsacid line lived on through the Armenian kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> However, not only did the Arsacid line continue through the Armenians, it as well continued through the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgian</a> kings with the <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_dynasty_of_Iberia" title="Arsacid dynasty of Iberia">Arsacid dynasty of Iberia</a>, and for many centuries afterwards in <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Albania" title="Caucasian Albania">Caucasian Albania</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arsacid_Dynasty_of_Caucasian_Albania" title="Arsacid Dynasty of Caucasian Albania">Arsacid Dynasty of Caucasian Albania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vardanes_II_of_Parthia" title="Vardanes II of Parthia">Vardanes II of Parthia</a> rebelled against his father Vologeses I in 55&#160;AD, Vologeses withdrew his forces from Armenia. Rome quickly attempted to fill the political vacuum left behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_58%E2%80%9363" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63</a>&#160;AD, the commander <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Domitius_Corbulo" title="Gnaeus Domitius Corbulo">Gnaeus Domitius Corbulo</a> achieved some military successes against the Parthians while installing <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_VI_of_Armenia" title="Tigranes VI of Armenia">Tigranes VI of Armenia</a> as a Roman client.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> However, Corbulo\'s successor <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Caesennius_Paetus" title="Lucius Caesennius Paetus">Lucius Caesennius Paetus</a> was soundly defeated by Parthian forces and fled Armenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Following a peace treaty, Tiridates I traveled to <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> and Rome in 63 AD. At both sites the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;54&#8211;68&#160;AD) ceremoniously crowned him king of Armenia by placing the royal <a href="/wiki/Diadem" title="Diadem">diadem</a> on his head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A long period of peace between Parthia and Rome ensued, with only the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a> into Parthia\'s eastern territories around 72&#160;AD mentioned by Roman historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Whereas Augustus and Nero had chosen a cautious military policy when confronting Parthia, later Roman emperors invaded and attempted to conquer the eastern <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, the heart of the Parthian Empire along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tigris_River" title="Tigris River">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>. The heightened aggression can be explained in part by Rome\'s military reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> To match Parthia\'s strength in missile troops and mounted warriors, the Romans at first used foreign allies (especially <a href="/wiki/Nabataeans" title="Nabataeans">Nabataeans</a>), but later established a permanent <i><a href="/wiki/Auxilia" title="Auxilia">auxilia</a></i> force to complement their heavy legionary infantry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_1996_87_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_1996_87-136">[136]</a></sup> The Romans eventually maintained regiments of horse archers (<i><a href="/wiki/Sagittarii" title="Sagittarii">sagittarii</a></i>) and even mail-armored <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphracts</a> in their eastern provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Yet the Romans had no discernible <a href="/wiki/Grand_strategy" title="Grand strategy">grand strategy</a> in dealing with Parthia and gained very little territory from these invasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The primary motivations for war were the advancement of the personal glory and political position of the emperor, as well as defending Roman honor against perceived slights such as Parthian interference in the affairs of Rome\'s client states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ParthianInChains.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1324" data-file-width="971" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/ParthianInChains.jpg/220px-ParthianInChains.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/ParthianInChains.jpg/330px-ParthianInChains.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/ParthianInChains.jpg/440px-ParthianInChains.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ParthianInChains.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Parthian (right) wearing a <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_cap" title="Phrygian cap">Phrygian cap</a>, depicted as a prisoner of war in chains held by a Roman (left); <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Septimius_Severus" title="Arch of Septimius Severus">Arch of Septimius Severus</a>, Rome, 203&#160;AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Hostilities between Rome and Parthia were renewed when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osroes_I_of_Parthia" title="Osroes I of Parthia">Osroes I of Parthia</a> (<i>r</i>. c.&#160;109&#8211;128&#160;AD) deposed the Armenian king <a href="/wiki/Sanatruk" title="Sanatruk">Sanatruk</a> and replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Axidares_of_Armenia" title="Axidares of Armenia">Axidares</a>, son of Pacorus II, without consulting Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;98&#8211;117&#160;AD) had the next Parthian nominee for the throne, Parthamasiris, killed in 114&#160;AD, instead making Armenia a Roman province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> His forces, led by <a href="/wiki/Lusius_Quietus" title="Lusius Quietus">Lusius Quietus</a>, also captured Nisibis; its occupation was essential to securing all the major routes across the northern Mesopotamian plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The following year, Trajan invaded Mesopotamia and met little resistance from only <a href="/wiki/Meharaspes" title="Meharaspes">Meharaspes</a> of Adiabene, since Osroes was engaged in a civil war to the east with <a href="/wiki/Vologases_III_of_Parthia" title="Vologases III of Parthia">Vologases III of Parthia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Trajan spent the winter of 115&#8211;116 at Antioch, but resumed his campaign in the spring. Marching down the Euphrates, he captured Dura-Europos, the capital Ctesiphon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and Seleucia, and even subjugated Characene, where he watched ships depart to India from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg/150px-Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg/225px-Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg/300px-Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_warrior_Termez_Achaeological_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parthian warrior, c. 100 BC&#8211;100 AD. <a href="/wiki/Termez" title="Termez">Termez</a> Achaeological Museum, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the last months of 116&#160;AD, Trajan captured the Persian city of Susa. When <a href="/wiki/Sanatruces_II_of_Parthia" title="Sanatruces II of Parthia">Sanatruces II of Parthia</a> gathered forces in eastern Parthia to challenge the Romans, his cousin <a href="/wiki/Parthamaspates_of_Parthia" title="Parthamaspates of Parthia">Parthamaspates of Parthia</a> betrayed and killed him: Trajan crowned him the new king of Parthia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bivar_1983_90-91_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-bivar_1983_90-91-146">[146]</a></sup> Never again would the Roman Empire advance so far to the east. On Trajan\'s return north, the Babylonian settlements revolted against the Roman garrisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Trajan was forced to retreat from Mesopotamia in 117&#160;AD, overseeing a failed siege of <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a> during his withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> His retreat was&#8212;in his intentions&#8212;temporary, because he wanted to renew the attack on Parthia in 118 AD and "make the subjection of the Parthians a reality,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> but Trajan died suddenly in August 117&#160;AD. During his campaign, Trajan was granted the title <i>Parthicus</i> by the Senate and coins were minted proclaiming the conquest of Parthia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> However, only the 4th-century AD historians <a href="/wiki/Eutropius_(historian)" title="Eutropius (historian)">Eutropius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Festus_(historian)" title="Festus (historian)">Festus</a> allege that he attempted to establish a <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia_(Roman_province)" title="Mesopotamia (Roman province)">Roman province</a> in lower Mesopotamia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Trajan\'s successor <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;117&#8211;138&#160;AD) reaffirmed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman-Parthian_Wars" title="Roman-Parthian Wars">Roman-Parthian</a> border at the Euphrates, choosing not to invade Mesopotamia due to Rome\'s now limited military resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Parthamaspates fled after the Parthians revolted against him, yet the Romans made him king of <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>. Osroes I died during his conflict with Vologases III, the latter succeeded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vologases_IV_of_Parthia" title="Vologases IV of Parthia">Vologases IV of Parthia</a> (<i>r</i>. c.&#160;147&#8211;191&#160;AD) who ushered in a period of peace and stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_161%E2%80%93166" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 161&#8211;166">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 161&#8211;166</a>&#160;AD began when Vologases invaded Armenia and Syria, retaking Edessa. Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;161&#8211;180&#160;AD) had co-ruler <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;161&#8211;169&#160;AD) guard Syria while <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Statius_Priscus" title="Marcus Statius Priscus">Marcus Statius Priscus</a> invaded Armenia in 163&#160;AD, followed by the invasion of Mesopotamia by <a href="/wiki/Avidius_Cassius" title="Avidius Cassius">Avidius Cassius</a> in 164&#160;AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The Romans captured and burnt Seleucia and Ctesiphon to the ground, yet they were forced to retreat once the Roman soldiers contracted a <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">deadly disease</a> (possibly <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>) that soon ravaged the Roman world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Although they withdrew, from this point forward the city of Dura-Europos remained in Roman hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> When Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;193&#8211;211&#160;AD) invaded Mesopotamia in 197&#160;AD during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vologases_V_of_Parthia" title="Vologases V of Parthia">Vologases V of Parthia</a> (<i>r</i>. c.&#160;191&#8211;208&#160;AD), the Romans once again marched down the Euphrates and captured Seleucia and Ctesiphon. After assuming the title <i>Parthicus Maximus</i>, he retreated in late 198&#160;AD, failing as Trajan once did to capture Hatra during a siege.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg/220px-Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg/330px-Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg/440px-Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bas_relief_nagsh-e-rostam_couronnement.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid</a> relief at <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a> showing the <a href="/wiki/Investiture" title="Investiture">investiture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Around 212&#160;AD, soon after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vologases_VI_of_Parthia" title="Vologases VI of Parthia">Vologases VI of Parthia</a> (<i>r</i>. c.&#160;208&#8211;222&#160;AD) took the throne, his brother <a href="/wiki/Artabanus_V_of_Parthia" title="Artabanus V of Parthia">Artabanus V of Parthia</a> (d.&#160;224&#160;AD) rebelled against him and gained control over a greater part of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bivar_1983_94-95_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-bivar_1983_94-95-158">[158]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;211&#8211;217&#160;AD) deposed the kings of Osroene and Armenia to make them Roman provinces once more. He marched into Mesopotamia under the pretext of marrying one of Artabanus\' daughters, but&#8212;because the marriage was not allowed&#8212;made war on Parthia and conquered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbil" title="Arbil">Arbil</a> east of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bivar_1983_94-95_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-bivar_1983_94-95-158">[158]</a></sup> Caracalla was assassinated the next year on the road to Carrhae by his soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bivar_1983_94-95_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-bivar_1983_94-95-158">[158]</a></sup> After this debacle, the Parthians made a settlement with <a href="/wiki/Macrinus" title="Macrinus">Macrinus</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;217&#8211;218) where the Romans paid Parthia over two-hundred million <i><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarii</a></i> with additional gifts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Parthian Empire, weakened by internal strife and wars with Rome, was soon to be followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a>. Indeed, shortly afterward, <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a>, the local Iranian ruler of Persis (modern <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Fars Province</a>, Iran) from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estakhr" title="Estakhr">Estakhr</a> began subjugating the surrounding territories in defiance of Arsacid rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_101_bivar_1983_95-96_curtis_2007_14_katouzian_2009_44_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_101_bivar_1983_95-96_curtis_2007_14_katouzian_2009_44-160">[160]</a></sup> He confronted Artabanus V at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hormozdg%C4%81n" title="Battle of Hormozdg&#257;n">Battle of Hormozdg&#257;n</a> on 28 April 224&#160;AD, perhaps at a site near <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, defeating him and establishing the Sassanid Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_101_bivar_1983_95-96_curtis_2007_14_katouzian_2009_44_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_101_bivar_1983_95-96_curtis_2007_14_katouzian_2009_44-160">[160]</a></sup> There is evidence, however, that suggests Vologases VI continued to mint coins at Seleucia as late as 228&#160;AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sassanians would not only assume Parthia\'s legacy as Rome\'s Persian nemesis, but they would also attempt to restore the boundaries of the Achaemenid Empire by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine-Sassanid_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine-Sassanid War of 602&#8211;628">briefly conquering</a> the Levant, Anatolia, and Egypt from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khosrau_II" title="Khosrau II">Khosrau II</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;590&#8211;628&#160;AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> However, they would lose these territories to <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a>&#8212;the last Roman emperor before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Arab conquests</a>. Nevertheless, for a period of more than 400 years, they succeeded the Parthian realm as Rome\'s principal rival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIr-Sasanian_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIr-Sasanian-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_and_external_sources">Native and external sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Native and external sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:128px;max-width:128px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="781" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg/126px-Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg/189px-Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg/252px-Parthian_gold_funerary_objects_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg 2x" width="126"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:176px;max-width:176px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg/174px-Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg/261px-Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg/348px-Parthian_jewelry_from_Nineveh_by_Nickmard_Khoey.jpg 2x" width="174"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Parthian gold jewelry items found at a burial site in <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> (near modern <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Local and foreign written accounts, as well as non-textual artifacts, have been used to reconstruct Parthian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1261-1262_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1261-1262-166">[166]</a></sup> Although the Parthian court maintained records, the Parthians had no formal study of <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>; the earliest <a href="/wiki/Universal_history" title="Universal history">universal history</a> of Iran, the <i><a href="/wiki/Khwaday-Namag" title="Khwaday-Namag">Khwaday-Namag</a></i>, was not compiled until the reign of the last Sassanid ruler <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_III" title="Yazdegerd III">Yazdegerd III</a> (r. 632&#8211;651&#160;AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Indigenous <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">sources</a> on Parthian history remain scarce, with fewer of them available than for any other period of Iranian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1261_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1261-168">[168]</a></sup> Most contemporary written records on Parthia contain Greek as well as Parthian and Aramaic inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garthwaite_2005_75-76_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-garthwaite_2005_75-76-169">[169]</a></sup> The Parthian language was written in a distinct script derived from the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet#Achaemenid_period" title="Aramaic alphabet">Imperial Aramaic chancellery script</a> of the Achaemenids, and later developed into the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_scripts" title="Pahlavi scripts">Pahlavi writing system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png/220px-Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png/330px-Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png/440px-Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Samartian-Persian_necklace_and_amulet.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sarmatian-Parthian gold necklace and amulet, 2nd century AD. Located in Tamoikin Art Fund</div></div></div>\n<p>The most valuable indigenous sources for reconstructing an accurate chronology of Arsacid rulers are the metal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drachma" title="Drachma">drachma</a> coins issued by each ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> These represent a "transition from non-textual to textual remains," according to historian <a href="/wiki/Geo_Widengren" title="Geo Widengren">Geo Widengren</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1262_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1262-172">[172]</a></sup> Other Parthian sources used for reconstructing chronology include <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> astronomical tablets and colophons discovered in Babylonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1265_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1265-173">[173]</a></sup> Indigenous textual sources also include stone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inscription" title="Inscription">inscriptions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papyri" title="Papyri">papyri</a> documents, and pottery <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostraca" title="Ostraca">ostraca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1262_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1262-172">[172]</a></sup> For example, at the early Parthian capital of Mithradatkert/Nisa in Turkmenistan, large caches of pottery ostraca have been found yielding information on the sale and storage of items like wine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Along with parchment documents found at sites like Dura-Europos, these also provide valuable information on Parthian governmental administration, covering issues such as taxation, military titles, and provincial organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2333" data-file-width="2875" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG/220px-Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG/330px-Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG/440px-Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Necklace_-_Parthian_Empire_2nd_AD.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parthian golden necklace, 2nd century AD, Iran, <a href="/wiki/Reza_Abbasi_Museum" title="Reza Abbasi Museum">Reza Abbasi Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iran-bastan-32.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="840" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Iran-bastan-32.jpg/220px-Iran-bastan-32.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Iran-bastan-32.jpg/330px-Iran-bastan-32.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Iran-bastan-32.jpg/440px-Iran-bastan-32.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iran-bastan-32.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Parthian ceramic <a href="/wiki/Oil_lamp" title="Oil lamp">oil lamp</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kh%C5%ABzest%C4%81n_Province" title="Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province">Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province</a>, Iran, <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Iran" title="National Museum of Iran">National Museum of Iran</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Greek_historiography" title="Greek historiography">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_historiography" title="Roman historiography">Latin histories</a>, which represent the majority of materials covering Parthian history, are not considered entirely reliable since they were written from the perspective of rivals and wartime enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> These external sources generally concern major military and political events, and often ignore social and cultural aspects of Parthian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The Romans usually depicted the Parthians as fierce warriors but also as a culturally refined people; recipes for Parthian dishes in the cookbook <i><a href="/wiki/Apicius" title="Apicius">Apicius</a></i> exemplifies their admiration for Parthian cuisine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Apollodorus_of_Artemita" title="Apollodorus of Artemita">Apollodorus of Artemita</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a> wrote histories focusing on Parthia, which are now lost and survive only as quoted extracts in other histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Charax" title="Isidore of Charax">Isidore of Charax</a>, who lived during the reign of Augustus, provides an account of Parthian territories, perhaps from a Parthian government survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1264_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1264-180">[180]</a></sup> To a lesser extent, people and events of Parthian history were also included in the histories of <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parthian history can also be reconstructed via the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Chinese historical records</a> of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1265,_1267_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1265,_1267-182">[182]</a></sup> In contrast to Greek and Roman histories, the early Chinese histories maintained a more neutral view when describing Parthia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> although the habit of Chinese chroniclers to copy material for their accounts from older works (of undetermined origin) makes it difficult to establish a chronological order of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> The Chinese called Parthia <i><a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%AE%89%E6%81%AF" title="zh:&#23433;&#24687;">&#256;nx&#299;</a></i> (Chinese: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%89" title="wiktionary:&#23433;">&#23433;</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%81%AF" title="wiktionary:&#24687;">&#24687;</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese_phonology" title="Old Chinese phonology">Old Chinese pronunciation</a>: \'<i>ansj&#601;k</i>), perhaps after the Greek name for the Parthian city <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Antiochia in Margiana</a> (Greek: &#7944;&#957;&#964;&#953;&#972;&#967;&#949;&#953;&#945; &#7969; &#7952;&#957; &#964;&#8135; &#924;&#945;&#961;&#947;&#953;&#945;&#957;&#8135;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> However, this could also have been a transliteration of "Arsaces", after the dynasty\'s eponymous founder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The works and historical authors include the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Shiji</a></i> (also known as the <i>Records of the Grand Historian</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Han shu</a></i> (Book of Han) by <a href="/wiki/Ban_Biao" title="Ban Biao">Ban Biao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ban_Gu" title="Ban Gu">Ban Gu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ban_Zhao" title="Ban Zhao">Ban Zhao</a>, and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Later_Han" title="Book of Later Han">Hou Han shu</a></i> (Book of Later Han) by <a href="/wiki/Fan_Ye_(historian)" title="Fan Ye (historian)">Fan Ye</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> They provide information on the nomadic migrations leading up to the early <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> invasion of Parthia and valuable political and geographical information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1265,_1267_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1265,_1267-182">[182]</a></sup> For example, the <i>Shiji</i> (ch. 123) describes diplomatic exchanges, exotic gifts given by Mithridates II to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han court</a>, types of agricultural crops grown in Parthia, production of wine using grapes, itinerant merchants, and the size and location of Parthian territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The <i>Shiji</i> also mentions that the Parthians kept records by "writing horizontally on strips of leather," that is, parchment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_administration">Government and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Government and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_authority_and_semi-autonomous_kings">Central authority and semi-autonomous kings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Central authority and semi-autonomous kings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:650521.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/650521.jpg/220px-650521.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/650521.jpg/330px-650521.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/650521.jpg/440px-650521.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:650521.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Kamnaskires_III" title="Kamnaskires III">Kamnaskires III</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Elymais" title="Elymais">Elymais</a> (modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kh%C5%ABzest%C4%81n_Province" title="Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province">Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province</a>), and his wife Queen <a href="/wiki/Anzaze" title="Anzaze">Anzaze</a>, 1st century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>Compared with the earlier Achaemenid Empire, the Parthian government was notably <a href="/wiki/Decentralization" title="Decentralization">decentralized</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> An indigenous historical source reveals that territories overseen by the central government were organized in a similar manner to the Seleucid Empire. They both had a threefold division for their provincial hierarchies: the Parthian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marzb%C4%81n" title="Marzb&#257;n">marzb&#257;n</a>, x&#353;atrap, and dizpat, similar to the Seleucid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satrapy" title="Satrapy">satrapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eparchy" title="Eparchy">eparchy</a>, and hyparchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widengren_1983_1263_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-widengren_1983_1263-191">[191]</a></sup> The Parthian Empire also contained several subordinate semi-autonomous kingdoms, including the states of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasian_Iberia" title="Caucasian Iberia">Caucasian Iberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atropatene" title="Atropatene">Atropatene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gordyene" title="Gordyene">Gordyene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edessa,_Mesopotamia" title="Edessa, Mesopotamia">Edessa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesene" title="Mesene">Mesene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elymais" title="Elymais">Elymais</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Persis" title="Kings of Persis">Persis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lukonin_1983_701_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-lukonin_1983_701-192">[192]</a></sup> The state rulers governed their own territories and minted their own coinage distinct from the royal coinage produced at the imperial mints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> This was not unlike the earlier Achaemenid Empire, which also had some city-states, and even distant satrapies who were semi-independent but "recognised the supremacy of the king, paid tribute and provided military support", according to Brosius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> However, the satraps of Parthian times governed smaller territories, and perhaps had less prestige and influence than their Achaemenid predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> During the Seleucid period, the trend of local ruling dynasties with semi-autonomous rule, and sometimes outright rebellious rule, became commonplace, a fact reflected in the later Parthian style of governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nobility">Nobility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nobility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Parthian_clans" title="Seven Parthian clans">Seven Parthian clans</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1189" data-file-width="1026" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg/220px-Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg/330px-Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg/440px-Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arm_less_man_edit_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Statue,_National_Museum_of_Iran_2401" title="Statue, National Museum of Iran 2401">bronze statue</a> of a Parthian nobleman from the sanctuary at Shami in <a href="/wiki/Elymais" title="Elymais">Elymais</a> (modern-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kh%C5%ABzest%C4%81n_Province" title="Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province">Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, along the Persian Gulf), now located at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Iran" title="National Museum of Iran">National Museum of Iran</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The King of Kings headed the Parthian government. He maintained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygamous" title="Polygamous">polygamous</a> relations, and was usually succeeded by his first-born son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_103-104_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_103-104-197">[197]</a></sup> Like the Ptolemies of Egypt, there is also record of Arsacid kings marrying their nieces and perhaps even half-sisters; Queen Musa married her own son, though this was an extreme and isolated case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_103-104_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_103-104-197">[197]</a></sup> Brosius provides an extract from a letter written in Greek by King Artabanus II in 21&#160;AD, which addresses the governor (titled "<a href="/wiki/Archon" title="Archon">archon</a>") and citizens of the city of <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>. Specific government offices of Preferred Friend, Bodyguard and Treasurer are mentioned and the document also proves that "while there were local jurisdictions and proceedings to appointment to high office, the king could intervene on behalf of an individual, review a case and amend the local ruling if he considered it appropriate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The hereditary titles of the hierarchic nobility recorded during the reign of the first Sassanid monarch Ardashir I most likely reflect the titles already in use during the Parthian era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> There were three distinct tiers of nobility, the highest being the regional kings directly below the King of Kings, the second being those related to the King of Kings only through marriage, and the lowest order being heads of local clans and small territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the 1st century AD, the Parthian nobility had assumed great power and influence in the succession and deposition of Arsacid kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Some of the nobility functioned as court advisers to the king, as well as holy priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographica</a></i>, preserved a claim by the Greek philosopher and historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poseidonius" title="Poseidonius">Poseidonius</a> that the Council of Parthia consisted of noble kinsmen and <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">magi</a>, two groups from which "the kings were appointed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Of the great noble Parthian families listed at the beginning of the Sassanian period, only two are explicitly mentioned in earlier Parthian documents: the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Suren" title="House of Suren">House of Suren</a> and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Karen" title="House of Karen">House of Karen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> noted that members of the Suren family, the first among the nobility, were given the privilege of crowning each new Arsacid King of Kings during their coronations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Later on, some of the Parthian kings would claim Achaemenid descent. This has recently been corroborated via the possibility of an inherited disease (<a href="/wiki/Neurofibromatosis" title="Neurofibromatosis">neurofibromatosis</a>) demonstrated by the physical descriptions of rulers and from evidence of familial disease on ancient coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Parthian Empire had no <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a>, yet were able to quickly recruit troops in the event of local crises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> There was a permanent armed guard attached to the person of the king, comprising nobles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serf" title="Serf">serfs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercenaries" title="Mercenaries">mercenaries</a>, but this royal retinue was small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_1996_84_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_1996_84-208">[208]</a></sup> Garrisons were also permanently maintained at border forts; Parthian inscriptions reveal some of the military titles granted to the commanders of these locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_1996_84_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_1996_84-208">[208]</a></sup> Military forces could also be used in diplomatic gestures. For example, when Chinese envoys visited Parthia in the late 2nd century BC, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i> maintains that 20,000 horsemen were sent to the eastern borders to serve as escorts for the embassy, although this figure is perhaps an exaggeration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:491px;max-width:491px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:155px;max-width:155px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:167px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ParthianHorseman.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="478" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/ParthianHorseman.jpg/153px-ParthianHorseman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/ParthianHorseman.jpg/230px-ParthianHorseman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/ParthianHorseman.jpg/306px-ParthianHorseman.jpg 2x" width="153"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Parthian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_archer" title="Horse archer">horse archer</a>, now on display at the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Madama,_Turin" title="Palazzo Madama, Turin">Palazzo Madama, Turin</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:209px;max-width:209px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:167px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ParthianCataphract.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1162" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/ParthianCataphract.JPG/207px-ParthianCataphract.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/ParthianCataphract.JPG/311px-ParthianCataphract.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/ParthianCataphract.JPG/414px-ParthianCataphract.JPG 2x" width="207"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Parthian <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphract</a> fighting a lion</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:121px;max-width:121px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:167px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1740" data-file-width="1236" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG/119px-Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG/179px-Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG/238px-Zahhak_castle_stucco_2.JPG 2x" width="119"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A Parthian <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a> relief of an infantryman, from the walls of <a href="/wiki/Zahhak_Castle" title="Zahhak Castle">Zahhak Castle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Azarbaijan_Province" title="East Azarbaijan Province">East Azarbaijan Province</a>, Iran</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">The combination of horse archers and cataphracts formed an effective backbone for the Parthian military</div></div></div>\n<p>The main striking force of the Parthian army was its <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphracts</a>, heavy cavalry with man and horse decked in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mail_(armour)" title="Mail (armour)">mailed armor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_120_garthwaite_2005_78_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_120_garthwaite_2005_78-210">[210]</a></sup> The cataphracts were equipped with a lance for charging into enemy lines, but were not equipped with bows and arrows which were restricted to horse archers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Due to the cost of their equipment and armor, cataphracts were recruited from among the aristocratic class who, in return for their services, demanded a measure of autonomy at the local level from the Arsacid kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> The light cavalry was recruited from among the commoner class and acted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_archer" title="Horse archer">horse archers</a>; they wore a simple tunic and trousers into battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_120_garthwaite_2005_78_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_120_garthwaite_2005_78-210">[210]</a></sup> They used <a href="/wiki/Composite_bow" title="Composite bow">composite bows</a> and were able to shoot at enemies while riding and facing away from them; this technique, known as the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_shot" title="Parthian shot">Parthian shot</a>, was a highly effective tactic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup>\nThe heavy and light cavalry of Parthia proved to be a decisive factor in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a> where a Parthian force defeated a much larger Roman army under Crassus.\nLight infantry units, composed of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">levied commoners</a> and mercenaries, were used to disperse enemy troops after cavalry charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The size of the Parthian army is unknown, as is the size of the empire\'s overall population. However, archaeological excavations in former Parthian urban centers reveal settlements which could have sustained large populations and hence a great resource in manpower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_1996_83_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_1996_83-215">[215]</a></sup> Dense population centers in regions like Babylonia were no doubt attractive to the Romans, whose armies could afford to live off the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_1996_83_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_1996_83-215">[215]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency">Currency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Currency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Usually made of silver,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Greek_drachma" title="Greek drachma">Greek drachma</a> coin, including the <a href="/wiki/Tetradrachm" title="Tetradrachm">tetradrachm</a>, was the standard currency used throughout the Parthian Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> The Arsacids maintained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" title="Mint (coin)">royal mints</a> at the cities of Hecatompylos, Seleucia, and Ecbatana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_103_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_103-40">[40]</a></sup> They most likely operated a mint at Mithridatkert/Nisa as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtis_2007_8_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-curtis_2007_8-24">[24]</a></sup> From the empire\'s inception until its collapse, drachmas produced throughout the Parthian period rarely weighed less than 3.5&#160;g or more than 4.2&#160;g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> The first Parthian tetradrachms, weighing in principle around 16&#160;g with some variation, appear after Mithridates I conquered Mesopotamia and were minted exclusively at Seleucia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenism_and_the_Iranian_revival">Hellenism and the Iranian revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hellenism and the Iranian revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg/220px-Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg/330px-Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg/440px-Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drachma_Mithradates_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_II_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates II of Parthia">Mithridates II of Parthia</a>. The clothing is Parthian, while the style is Hellenistic (sitting on an <a href="/wiki/Omphalos" title="Omphalos">omphalos</a>). The Greek inscription reads "King Arsaces, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philhellene" title="Philhellene">philhellene</a>"</div></div></div>\n<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> of the Seleucids was widely adopted by peoples of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, the Parthian era witnessed an <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Iranian cultural revival</a> in religion, the arts, and even clothing fashions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Conscious of both the Hellenistic and Persian cultural roots of their kingship, the Arsacid rulers styled themselves after the Persian King of Kings and affirmed that they were also <i><a href="/wiki/Philhellenism" title="Philhellenism">philhellenes</a></i> ("friends of the Greeks").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> The word "philhellene" was inscribed on Parthian coins until the reign of Artabanus II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtis_2007_11_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-curtis_2007_11-222">[222]</a></sup> The discontinuation of this phrase signified the revival of Iranian culture in Parthia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtis_2007_16_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-curtis_2007_16-223">[223]</a></sup> Vologases I was the first Arsacid ruler to have the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pahlavi_script" title="Pahlavi script">Parthian script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">language</a> appear on his minted coins alongside the now almost illegible Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> However, the use of Greek-alphabet legends on Parthian coins remained until the collapse of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="818" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg/220px-ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg/330px-ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg/440px-ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ParthianWaterSpoutWithFaceOfIranianMan1-2ndCenturyCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A ceramic Parthian water spout in the shape of a man\'s head, dated 1st or 2nd century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Greek cultural influence did not disappear from the Parthian Empire, however, and there is evidence that the Arsacids enjoyed <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Greece" title="Theatre of ancient Greece">Greek theatre</a>. When the head of Crassus was brought to Orodes II, he, alongside Armenian king Artavasdes II, were busy watching a performance of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bacchae" title="The Bacchae">The Bacchae</a></i> by the playwright <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a> (c.&#160;480&#8211;406 BC). The producer of the play decided to use Crassus\' actual severed head in place of the <a href="/wiki/Theatrical_property" title="Theatrical property">stage-prop</a> head of <a href="/wiki/Pentheus" title="Pentheus">Pentheus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On his coins, Arsaces I is depicted in apparel similar to Achaemenid satraps. According to A. Shahbazi, Arsaces "deliberately diverges from Seleucid coins to emphasize his nationalistic and royal aspirations, and he calls himself K&#257;rny/Karny (Greek: Autocrator), a title already borne by Achaemenid supreme generals, such as Cyrus the Younger."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shahbazi525_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-shahbazi525-227">[227]</a></sup> In line with Achaemenid traditions, rock-relief images of Arsacid rulers were carved at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Behistun" title="Mount Behistun">Mount Behistun</a>, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius I of Persia</a> (r. 522&#8211;486 BC) made <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">royal inscriptions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Moreover, the Arsacids claimed familial descent from <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II_of_Persia" title="Artaxerxes II of Persia">Artaxerxes II of Persia</a> (<i>r</i>.&#160;404&#8211;358 BC) as a means to bolster their legitimacy in ruling over former Achaemenid territories, i.e. as being "legitimate successors of glorious kings" of ancient Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artabanus_III" title="Artabanus III">Artabanus III</a> named one of his sons Darius and laid claim to <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus</a>\' heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shahbazi525_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-shahbazi525-227">[227]</a></sup> The Arsacid kings chose typical Zoroastrian names for themselves and some from the "<a href="/wiki/Kayanian_dynasty" title="Kayanian dynasty">heroic background</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>, according to V.G. Lukonin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> The Parthians also adopted the use of the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_calendar" title="Babylonian calendar">Babylonian calendar</a> with names from the Achaemenid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_calendar" title="Iranian calendar">Iranian calendar</a>, replacing the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_calendar" title="Ancient Macedonian calendar">Macedonian calendar</a> of the Seleucids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2137" data-file-width="1218" decoding="async" height="386" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg/220px-ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg/330px-ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg/440px-ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ParthianVotiveReliefIranKhuzestan2ndCenturyCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parthian votive relief from Kh&#363;zest&#257;n Province, Iran, 2nd century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>The Parthian Empire, being culturally and politically heterogeneous, had a variety of religious systems and beliefs, the most widespread being those dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Iranian</a> cults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katouzian_2009_45_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-katouzian_2009_45-232">[232]</a></sup> Aside from a minority of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jews</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> and early <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> most Parthians were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_125_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_125-235">[235]</a></sup> Greek and Iranian deities were often blended together as one. For example, <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> was often equated with <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angra_Mainyu" title="Angra Mainyu">Angra Mainyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a> with <a href="/wiki/Anahita" title="Anahita">Anahita</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Aside from the main gods and goddesses, each ethnic group and city had their own designated deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_125_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_125-235">[235]</a></sup> As with Seleucid rulers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> Parthian art indicates that the Arsacid kings viewed themselves as gods;
