this <a href="/wiki/Imperial_cult" title="Imperial cult">cult of the ruler</a> was perhaps the most widespread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The extent of Arsacid patronism of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> is debated in modern scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> The followers of <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> would have found the bloody sacrifices of some Parthian-era Iranian cults to be unacceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-katouzian_2009_45_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-katouzian_2009_45-232">[232]</a></sup> However, there is evidence that Vologeses I encouraged the presence of Zoroastrian <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">magi</a> priests at court and sponsored the compilation of sacred Zoroastrian texts which later formed the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> The Sassanid court would later adopt Zoroastrianism as the official state religion of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Mani_(prophet)" title="Mani (prophet)">Mani</a> (216&#8211;276 AD), the founding prophet of <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, did not proclaim his first religious revelation until 228/229 AD, Bivar asserts that his new faith contained "elements of <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaean</a> belief, Iranian cosmogony, and even echoes of Christianity&#160;... [it] may be regarded as a typical reflection of the mixed religious doctrines of the late Arsacid period, which the Zoroastrian orthodoxy of the Sasanians was soon to sweep away."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is scant archaeological evidence for the spread of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> into Iran proper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> However, it is known from Chinese sources that <a href="/wiki/An_Shigao" title="An Shigao">An Shigao</a> (fl. 2nd century AD), a Parthian nobleman and Buddhist monk, traveled to <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> in Han China as a <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">Buddhist missionary</a> and translated several <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts" title="Buddhist texts">Buddhist canons</a> into <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Parthian_art" title="Parthian art">Parthian art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hatra_(17).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hatra_%2817%29.jpg/220px-Hatra_%2817%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hatra_%2817%29.jpg/330px-Hatra_%2817%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hatra_%2817%29.jpg/440px-Hatra_%2817%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hatra_(17).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Barrel_vault" title="Barrel vault">barrel vaulted</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Iwan" title="Iwan">iwan</a></i> at the entrance at the ancient site of <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>, modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, built c.&#160;50 AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Parthian art can be divided into three geo-historical phases: the art of Parthia proper; the art of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_plateau" title="Iranian plateau">Iranian plateau</a>; and the art of Parthian Mesopotamia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_127_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_127-245">[245]</a></sup> The first genuine Parthian art, found at Mithridatkert/Nisa, combined elements of Greek and Iranian art in line with Achaemenid and Seleucid traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_127_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_127-245">[245]</a></sup> In the second phase, Parthian art found inspiration in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_art#Art_and_architecture" title="Achaemenid art">Achaemenid art</a>, as exemplified by the investiture relief of Mithridates II at Mount Behistun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_128_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_128-246">[246]</a></sup> The third phase occurred gradually after the Parthian conquest of Mesopotamia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_128_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_128-246">[246]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Common <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motifs</a> of the Parthian period include scenes of royal hunting expeditions and the <a href="/wiki/Investiture" title="Investiture">investiture</a> of Arsacid kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> Use of these motifs extended to include portrayals of local rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_127_245-2"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_127-245">[245]</a></sup> Common art mediums were rock-reliefs, <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescos</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Graffiti" title="Graffiti">graffiti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_127_245-3"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_127-245">[245]</a></sup> Geometric and stylized plant patterns were also used on <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plaster" title="Plaster">plaster</a> walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_128_246-2"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_128-246">[246]</a></sup> The common motif of the Sassanid period showing two horsemen engaged in combat with lances first appeared in the Parthian reliefs at Mount Behistun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Portrait" title="Portrait">portraiture</a> the Parthians favored and emphasized frontality, meaning the person depicted by painting, sculpture, or raised-relief on coins faced the viewer directly instead of showing his or her profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Although frontality in portraiture was already an old artistic technique by the Parthian period, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Schlumberger" title="Daniel Schlumberger">Daniel Schlumberger</a> explains the innovation of Parthian frontality:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schlumberger_1983_1051_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-schlumberger_1983_1051-250">[250]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>\'Parthian frontality\', as we are now accustomed to call it, deeply differs both from ancient Near Eastern and from Greek frontality, though it is, no doubt, an offspring of the latter. For both in Oriental art and in Greek art, frontality was an exceptional treatment: in Oriental art it was a treatment strictly reserved for a small number of traditional characters of cult and myth; in Greek art it was an option resorted to only for definite reasons, when demanded by the subject, and, on the whole, seldom made use of. With Parthian art, on the contrary, frontality becomes the normal treatment of the figure. For the Parthians frontality is really nothing but the habit of showing, in relief and in painting, all figures full-face, even at the expense (as it seems to us moderns) of clearness and intelligibility. So systematic is this use that it amounts to a complete banishment <i>de facto</i> of the side-view and of all intermediate attitudes. This singular state of things seems to have become established in the course of the 1st century A.D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schlumberger_1983_1051_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-schlumberger_1983_1051-250">[250]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duraeuropa-1-.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="716" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Duraeuropa-1-.gif/220px-Duraeuropa-1-.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Duraeuropa-1-.gif/330px-Duraeuropa-1-.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Duraeuropa-1-.gif/440px-Duraeuropa-1-.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duraeuropa-1-.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wall mural depicting a scene from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Book of Esther</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos_synagogue" title="Dura-Europos synagogue">Dura-Europos synagogue</a>, dated 245 AD, which Curtis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> and Schlumberger<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> describe as a fine example of \'Parthian frontality\'</div></div></div>\n<p>Parthian art, with its distinct use of frontality in portraiture, was lost and abandoned with the profound cultural and political changes brought by the Sassanid Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> However, even after the Roman occupation of Dura-Europos in 165 AD, the use of Parthian frontality in portraiture continued to flourish there. This is exemplified by the early 3rd-century AD wall murals of the <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos_synagogue" title="Dura-Europos synagogue">Dura-Europos synagogue</a>, a temple in the same city dedicated to Palmyrene gods, and the local <a href="/wiki/Mithraeum" title="Mithraeum">Mithraeum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parthian architecture adopted elements of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_architecture" title="Iranian architecture">Achaemenid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architecture_of_ancient_Greece" title="Architecture of ancient Greece">Greek architecture</a>, but remained distinct from the two. The style is first attested at Mithridatkert/Nisa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_111-112_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_111-112-255">[255]</a></sup> The Round Hall of Nisa is similar to Hellenistic palaces, but different in that it forms a circle and <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vault</a> inside a square space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_111-112_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_111-112-255">[255]</a></sup> However, the artwork of Nisa, including marble statues and the carved scenes on ivory <a href="/wiki/Rhyton" title="Rhyton">rhyton</a> vessels, is unquestionably influenced by Greek art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A signature feature of Parthian architecture was the <i><a href="/wiki/Iwan" title="Iwan">iwan</a></i>, an audience hall supported by arches or <a href="/wiki/Barrel_vault" title="Barrel vault">barrel vaults</a> and open on one side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garthwaite_2005_84_brosius_2006_128_schlumberger_1983_1049_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-garthwaite_2005_84_brosius_2006_128_schlumberger_1983_1049-257">[257]</a></sup> Use of the barrel vault replaced the Hellenic use of columns to support roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_128_246-3"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_128-246">[246]</a></sup> Although the <i>iwan</i> was known during the Achaemenid period and earlier in smaller and subterranean structures, it was the Parthians who first built them on a monumental scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garthwaite_2005_84_brosius_2006_128_schlumberger_1983_1049_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-garthwaite_2005_84_brosius_2006_128_schlumberger_1983_1049-257">[257]</a></sup> The earliest Parthian <i>iwans</i> are found at Seleucia, built in the early 1st century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_128_246-4"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_128-246">[246]</a></sup> Monumental <i>iwans</i> are also commonly found in the ancient temples of Hatra and perhaps modeled on the Parthian style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_134-135_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_134-135-258">[258]</a></sup> The largest Parthian <i>iwans</i> at that site have a span of 15&#160;m (50&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing_and_apparel">Clothing and apparel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Clothing and apparel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1159" data-file-width="548" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg/170px-YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg/255px-YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg/340px-YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YoungManWithParthianCostume.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of a young Palmyran in fine Parthian trousers, from a funerary <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>, early 3rd century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>The typical Parthian riding outfit is exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Statue,_National_Museum_of_Iran_2401" title="Statue, National Museum of Iran 2401">the famous bronze statue</a> of a Parthian nobleman found at Shami, Elymais. Standing 1.9&#160;m (6&#160;ft), the figure wears a V-shaped jacket, a V-shaped <a href="/wiki/Tunic" title="Tunic">tunic</a> fastened in place with a belt, loose-fitting and many-folded trousers held by garters, and a diadem or band over his coiffed, bobbed hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> His outfit is commonly seen in relief images of Parthian coins by the mid-1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtis_2007_16_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-curtis_2007_16-223">[223]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Examples of clothing in Parthian inspired sculptures have been found in excavations at Hatra, in northwestern Iraq. Statues erected there feature the typical Parthian shirt (<i>qamis</i>), combined with trousers and made with fine, ornamented materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> The aristocratic elite of Hatra adopted the bobbed hairstyles, headdresses, and belted tunics worn by the nobility belonging to the central Arsacid court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_134-135_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_134-135-258">[258]</a></sup> The trouser-suit was even worn by the Arsacid kings, as shown on the reverse images of coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> The Parthian trouser-suit was also adopted in <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>, Syria, along with the use of Parthian frontality in art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parthian sculptures depict wealthy women wearing long-sleeved robes over a dress, with necklaces, earrings, bracelets, and headdresses bedecked in jewelry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> Their many-folded dresses were fastened by a <a href="/wiki/Brooch" title="Brooch">brooch</a> at one shoulder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_134-135_258-2"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_134-135-258">[258]</a></sup> Their headdresses also featured a veil which was draped backwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_134-135_258-3"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_134-135-258">[258]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As seen in Parthian coinage, the headdresses worn by the Parthian kings changed over time. The earliest Arsacid coins show rulers wearing the soft cap with cheek flaps, known as the <a href="/wiki/Bashlyk" title="Bashlyk">bashlyk</a> (Greek: <i>kyrbasia</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brosius_2006_101_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-brosius_2006_101-265">[265]</a></sup> This may have derived from an Achaemenid-era satrapal headdress and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pointy_hat" title="Pointy hat">pointy hats</a> depicted in the Achaemenid reliefs at Behistun and <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> The earliest coins of Mithridates I show him wearing the soft cap, yet coins from the latter part of his reign show him for the first time wearing the royal Hellenistic diadem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> Mithridates II was the first to be shown wearing the Parthian <a href="/wiki/Tiara" title="Tiara">tiara</a>, embroidered with pearls and jewels, a headdress commonly worn in the late Parthian period and by Sassanid monarchs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As culturally and religiously tolerant as the Parthians were, they adopted <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> as their <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic_language" title="Old Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> remained the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> in the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_1992_45_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Green_1992_45-2">[2]</a></sup> The native <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">Parthian language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> were also used.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_and_literature">Writing and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parthian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Writing and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_lute_player.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Parthian_lute_player.jpg/220px-Parthian_lute_player.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Parthian_lute_player.jpg/330px-Parthian_lute_player.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Parthian_lute_player.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_lute_player.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parthian long-necked lute, c. 3 BC &#8212; 3 AD</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="353" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg/150px-Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg/225px-Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg/300px-Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sarbaz_Nysa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sculpted head (broken off from a larger statue) of a Parthian soldier wearing a <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_armies" title="Hellenistic armies">Hellenistic-style helmet</a>, from the Parthian royal residence and necropolis of <a href="/wiki/Nisa,_Turkmenistan" title="Nisa, Turkmenistan">Nisa, Turkmenistan</a>, 2nd century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>It is known that during the Parthian period the court <a href="/wiki/Minstrel" title="Minstrel">minstrel</a> (<i>g&#333;s&#257;n</i>) recited poetic <a href="/wiki/Oral_literature" title="Oral literature">oral literature</a> accompanied by music. However, their stories, composed in verse form, were not written down until the subsequent Sassanian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> In fact, there is no known Parthian-language literature that survives in original form, since it was written down in the following centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> It is believed that such stories as the romantic tale <i><a href="/wiki/Vis_and_R%C4%81min" title="Vis and R&#257;min">Vis and R&#257;min</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic cycle</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kayanian_dynasty" title="Kayanian dynasty">Kayanian dynasty</a> were part of the corpus of oral literature from Parthian times, although compiled much later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup> Although literature of the Parthian language was not committed to written form, there is evidence that the Arsacids acknowledged and respected written <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">Greek literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronological_table_of_Parthian_kings">Chronological table of Parthian kings</span><span kings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Parthian_kings" title="List of This was the local language of the area east of the Caspian Sea and official language of the Parthian state (see ARSACIDS) and is known from inscriptions on stone and metal, including coins and seals, and from large archives of potsherd labels on wine jars from the Parthian capital of Nisa, as well as from the Manichean texts.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.iranicaonline.org&amp;rft.atitle=IRAN+vi.+IRANIAN+LANGUAGES+AND+SCRIPTS+%282%29+Doc+%E2%80%93+Encyclopaedia+Iranica&amp;rft.aulast=Skjaervo&amp;rft.aufirst=Prods+Oktor&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iranicaonline.org%2Farticles%2Firan-vi2-documentation&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AParthian+Empire"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Michael_L._Chyet" title="Michael L. <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">Parthian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Humanism%2C+Culture%2C+and+Language+in+the+Near+East%3A+Studies+in+Honor+of+Georg+Krotkoff&amp;rft.pages=284&amp;rft.pub=Eisenbrauns&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-57506-020-0&amp;rft.aulast=Chyet&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael+L.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AParthian+Empire"></span><link title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a>. Each ethnic group, each city, and each land or kingdom was able to adhere to its own gods, their respective cults and religious rituals. In <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> the city-god <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a> continued to be the main deity alongside the goddesses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ishtar" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanaya" title="Nanaya">Nanai</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>\'s main god, the sun-god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>, was revered alongside a multiplicity of other gods.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Persians&amp;rft.pages=125&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-203-06815-1&amp;rft.aulast=Brosius&amp;rft.aufirst=Maria&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AParthian+Empire"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-sheldon_2010_231-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-sheldon_2010_231_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSheldon2010">Sheldon 2010</a>, p.&#160;231</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Turchin223-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Turchin223_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Turchin, Peter; Adams, Jonathan M.; Hall, Thomas D (December 2006). <a class="external text" href="http://jwsr.pitt.edu/ojs/index.php/jwsr/article/view/369/381" rel="nofollow">"East-West Orientation of Historical Empires"</a>. <i>Journal of world-systems research</i>. <b>12</b> (2): 223. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1076-156X" rel="nofollow">1076-156X</a><span class="reference-accessdate">.
